His statement: 'or he wore a sewn garment or covered his head for a day, otherwise he gives charity' is conjoined to 'perfume', explaining the second and third of the first type. He combined them because the ruling for both is the same in terms of temporal estimation. His statement 'a day' refers to both wearing and covering, and likewise his statement 'otherwise he gives charity' means if wearing a sewn garment or covering the head was less than a day, charity is incumbent upon him, because it is known that complete punishment is for complete offense, and complete offense is complete benefit, because the purpose of each is repelling heat and cold, and a day encompasses them. From his statement: 'and it appeared to me that if found...' Consider: can what was mentioned from al-Fath regarding the distinction between small and large amounts in clothing apply here? Then this distinction contradicts what we quoted from al-Fath that if perfume is dominant, expiation is obligatory even if its scent does not appear, for it necessitates that the criterion is abundance of parts, not presence of scent. Ponder. His statement: 'then he said...' means they obligated expiation when one eats or drinks from that in which perfume is dominant, and they did not differentiate between eating or drinking a small or large amount, and likewise when it is overpowered, but the mentioned differentiation is necessary, for it is far-fetched that a single morsel should obligate the same as a large amount. His statement: 'and he ate from it or drank much' the pronoun refers to the mixture with dominant perfume, whether food or drink. His statement: 'for in eating much is blood, and little is charity' said in al-Sharnublaliyyah: one should ponder the ruling on musk added to sweets with what we have mentioned about mixing it with food that is eaten and cooked, and when it is not cooked. For what preceded is that if he puts it in food and cooks, nothing is upon him; if he mixes it with food eaten without cooking, then if it is overpowered, nothing is upon him, and if it is dominant, expiation is obligatory even if its scent does not appear. On this basis, apparently this sweet is uncooked and its perfume is dominant to agree with what preceded. His statement: 'since it is known that punishment is for completeness of the offense...' its implication is that if he enters ihram for a ritual while wearing a sewn garment and performs that ritual completely in less than a day and exits ihram, that charity is incumbent upon him unless an explicit text exists to the contrary. If you say: being free of sewn garment in the ritual is obligatory absolutely, whether his ihram period is long or short, and the estimation by a day and night is only when the ihram period is long; as for when it is short, then blood is obligatory, and for a lesser offense charity is obligatory. The verification is that covering the head is part of wearing a sewn garment, so it is one offense, as will come that if he wears a shirt and turban, one blood is incumbent upon him; they reasoned that the offense is one. The reality of wearing a sewn garment is that by means of sewing, encompassing of the body and retention occurs. Hence if he wraps a shirt around himself or puts it on like a cloak or wears trousers as a waist-wrapper, there is no harm, because he did not wear it as a sewn garment due to lack of encompassing. Likewise if he inserts his shoulders into a cloak but does not insert his hands into the sleeves and does not button it, due to lack of encompassing. But if he inserts his hands or buttons it, it is wearing a sewn garment due to their existence, unlike the upper garment if he wears it as a waist-wrapper, he should not tie it with a rope or otherwise; even if he does, nothing is upon him because he did not wear it as a sewn garment due to lack of encompassing. He made 'wearing' absolute, so it includes whether he wears after ihram or enters ihram while wearing and continues. Unlike benefiting after ihram from perfume applied before ihram, due to the text; otherwise we would obligate it also. It includes whether he is forgetful, deliberate, knowledgeable, ignorant, voluntary, or coerced; expiation is obligatory on one who sleeps if someone covers his head, because benefit accrued to him, and lack of choice removes sin from him, like a sleeping person who rolls onto something he destroys. It includes whether he wears one garment or all garments together: shirt, turban, and socks. Hence he did not say 'wears a garment' like others. The author (al-Kanz) mentioned the ruling of a day and less, but did not mention the ruling of more, to indicate that it is like a day. So if he wears a sewn garment and continues for days, or removes it at night and repeats during the day or vice versa, one blood is incumbent upon him unless he intends to stop when removing. If he intends to stop and then wears again, expiation multiple, expiating for the first first time? And in the second is the disagreement of Muhammad. If he wears for a day and offers the blood, then continues wearing for another day, another blood is upon him without disagreement, because continuation has the ruling of initiation. And regarding: 'for him in his ritual there is complete benefit, so he would be leaving an obligation of his ihram, so should blood be obligatory?' I say: no doubt it is exquisite, but it needs an explicit transmission. This is a summary from the hashiyah of al-Madani from the commentary on al-Mansak by Shaykh Abd Allah al-Afif, and in it from the fatawa of his student al-Fadil Abd Allah Afandi Ataqi that he inclined to the obligation of blood. His statement: 'and the verification that covering the head...' said in al-Nahr: the verification is that between wearing a sewn garment and covering the head there is general and specific in absolute terms; they meet in covering like a sewn skullcap, and covering is unique by placing something like muslin that is not sewn on the head, and this suffices for the portion of difference. His statement: 'by means of sewing' is objected to by felt that is attached by adhesion, for it has no sewing yet it is considered sewn, unless by sewing is meant joining some parts to others (Sharh al-Lubab). His statement: 'or he gathered all the clothing' meaning in one sitting. Likewise in Sharh al-Lubab. Its implication is that if the sitting differs within one day, multiple expiations, and we will mention soon what contradicts it. His statement: 'as long as he did not intend to stop' i.e., he did not remove it with the intention to stop but removed it with the intention to wear again or removed it to replace it. Likewise in Sharh al-Lubab. It indicates that removing it to replace it does not make multiple expiations, so memorize, for it occurs often. His statement: 'and in the second' i.e., when he did not expiate for the first. His statement: 'and 'indi al-mudi'' in this manuscript with 'indi' added to the first person pronoun, and thus I saw it in...
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling for a muhrim who wears a sewn garment or covers his head. The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states that if done for a full day, a dam (blood sacrifice) is obligated; if less than a day, sadaqah (charity) is required. The commentator clarifies that this ruling applies to both actions equally because the underlying benefit (irtifaq) of protection from heat and cold is achieved fully by a full day's duration. The passage then explores various details: the definition of 'wearing a sewn garment' requires the garment to encompass the body and be held by sewing — wrapping or draping without proper wear is not considered wearing. It emphasizes that the ruling applies regardless of intent (forgetful, deliberate, ignorant, coerced) except for perfume applied before ihram, which is excluded by textual evidence. A significant portion addresses the case of multiple garments: wearing a shirt, turban, and socks together counts as one offense and requires only one dam, as long as done in one sitting. If the removal and re-wearing are done with the intention to permanently stop (and then later re-wear), multiple expiations are due; this is the position of Abu Hanifa, with a disagreement from Imam Muhammad. The text also discusses the scenario of continuous wearing across multiple days: if one wears for a day, offers the dam, and then continues wearing, another day requires another dam by consensus. There is also a discussion about a hypothetical case where a muhrim completes his entire ihram in less than a day while wearing a sewn garment — some scholars inclined to obligate blood, but the commentator notes this needs explicit textual support.

Key Terms
مخيط (makhīṭ): A sewn garment that encompasses the body and is held by stitching, as opposed to a draped or wrapped cloth.
ارتفاق (irtifāq): Benefit or protection achieved by wearing a garment or covering, specifically from heat and cold.
دم (dam): A blood sacrifice (usually a sheep) required as expiation for major infractions during ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does a muhrim incur multiple expiations for wearing a sewn garment?
A) Whenever he removes and re-wears any garment
B) Only if he removes with the intention to stop permanently and then re-wears
C) Only if the total duration exceeds two days
D) Only if he wears two different garments

Key Takeaway
The number of expiations for wearing sewn garments during ihram depends on the intention upon removal: if intended as permanent cessation, each subsequent wearing triggers a new expiation (Abu Hanifa's view).

Covering the Head and Wearing Sewn Garments in Ihram
Defining the violation of covering the head and wearing sewn garments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define labs al-makhit and distinguish between major and minor violations in ihram regarding covering the head and wearing sewn garments.

Core Idea
A pilgrim who wears a sewn garment enveloping the body must offer a dam; mere wrapping without envelopment is not a violation.

In this lesson, we analyze a passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that clarifies what constitutes a violation in ihram when covering the head or wearing sewn garments. The Hanafi school draws a precise line: a major violation (requiring a blood sacrifice) occurs only when the garment is sewn and worn in a way that envelopes the body part. Otherwise, the violation is minor and only charity is due. Understanding this distinction is key to properly expiating ihram infractions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels of violation
The text begins by stating that a blood sacrifice (dam) is obligated for a major violation, and charity (sadaqah) for a minor one. Recognize that the author then verifies that covering the head is part of wearing sewn garments, so it is a single major violation if the covering is sewn.
Major violations require a dam; minor violations require sadaqah.
2
Define labs al-makhit
The author defines the reality of wearing a sewn garment (labs al-makhit) as involving two elements: ishtimal (envelopment of the body) and istimsak (adherence resulting from sewing). Understand that both must be present for the act to be considered the prohibited wearing.
The word 'ish'timal' is key: the garment must fully enclose the body part.
3
Apply the definition to examples
The passage gives several applications: draping a shirt over the shoulders (no violation), inserting arms into sleeves (violation), wearing trousers as an izār (no violation), etc. Work through each example to see how the definition determines the ruling.
The examples illustrate the boundary between permissible and prohibited.
4
Understand the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies whether the pilgrim started wearing after ihram or was already wearing when entering ihram (continuous). It also applies to the forgetful, the coerced, and even to a sleeper whose head is covered by another, because comfort occurs. However, sin is removed due to lack of choice.
The obligation of compensation is tied to the occurrence of benefit (irtifāq), not to intention.
5
Recognize the unification of multiple garments
The text states that wearing a shirt and turban together requires only one dam, because all are considered a single violation. The author indicates that wearing one garment or multiple garments within the same continuous period is treated as one violation.
Multiple garments do not multiply the dam; the key is that the violation is one act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج باب الجنايات فوجب الدم والجناية قاصرة فيما دونه فوجبت الصدقة. والتحقيق أن تغطية الرأس من جملة لبس المخيط فهي جناية واحدة لما سيأتي أنه لو لبس القميص والعمامة يلزمه دم واحد، عللوا بأن الجناية واحدة وحقيقة لبس المخيط أن يحصل بواسطة الخياطة اشتمال على البدن واستمساك فلذا لو ارتدى بالقميص أو اتشح أو ائتزر بالسراويل فلا بأس به لأنه لم يلبسه لبس المخيط لعدم الاشتمال، وكذا لو أدخل منكبيه في القباء ولم يدخل يديه في الكمين ولم يزره لعدم الاشتمال، أما إذا أدخل يديه أو زره فهو لبس المخيط لوجودهما بخلاف الرداء فإنه إذا اتزر به لا ينبغي أن يعقده بحبل أو غيره، ومع هذا لو فعل لا شيء عليه لأنه لم يلبسه لبس المخيط لعدم الاشتمال. أطلق في اللبس فشمل ما إذا أحدث اللبس بعد الإحرام أو أحرم وهو لابسه فدام على ذلك بخلاف انتفاعه بعد الإحرام بالطيب السابق عليه قبله للنص ولولاه لأوجبنا فيه أيضاً. وشمل ما إذا كان ناسياً أو عامداً عالماً أو جاهلاً مختاراً أو مكرهاً فيجب الجزاء على النائم لو غطى إنسان رأسه لأن الارتفاق حصل له وعدم الاختيار أسقط الإثم عنه كالنائم المنقلب على شيء أتلفه وشمل ما إذا لبس ثوباً واحداً أو جمع اللباس كله القميص والعمامة والخفين ولذا لم يقل لبس ثوباً كغيره وبين المصنف حكم اليوم وما دونه ولم يذكر حكم الزائد عليه ليفيد أنه كاليوم.
Book of Hajj, Chapter of Violations. So the blood sacrifice is obligated, and the violation is minor below that so charity is obligated. The verification is that covering the head is part of wearing sewn garments, so it is a single violation, as will come that if he wears a shirt and a turban, one blood sacrifice is required of him. They reasoned that the violation is one, and the reality of wearing a sewn garment is that through sewing there occurs envelopment of the body and adherence. Hence if he wraps the shirt around his shoulders (like a cloak) or drapes it over his body (like a shawl) or wears trousers around his waist (like an izār) there is no harm, because he did not wear it as a sewn garment due to lack of envelopment. Similarly, if he inserts his shoulders into a cloak but does not insert his hands into the sleeves and does not button it, due to lack of envelopment. But if he inserts his hands or buttons it, it is wearing a sewn garment due to the presence of [both conditions]. This differs from a mantle (ridāʾ), for if he wears it as an izār, he should not tie it with a rope or other; even if he does, nothing is upon him because he did not wear it as a sewn garment due to lack of envelopment. And it includes whether he wears one garment or gathers all garments: the shirt, turban, and socks. Hence he did not say 'he wore a garment' like others. And the author clarified the ruling of a day and less than it, and did not mention the ruling of more than it to indicate that it is like a day.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a Hanafi commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, specifically on the chapter of Hajj violations. The author, Ibn Nujaym, begins by stating the basic rule: if a pilgrim commits a major violation (like covering the head with a sewn garment or wearing a sewn garment properly), he must offer a dam (blood sacrifice). If the violation is minor (like covering the head with an unsewn cloth), only sadaqah (charity) is due. Then he verifies that covering the head is part of wearing sewn garments because wearing sewn garments is defined by 'ishtimal' (enveloping the body) and 'istimsak' (adherence due to sewing). So if someone wears a garment in a way that does not envelope the body—like draping a shirt over the shoulders without inserting arms—it is not the prohibited 'wearing of sewn garments'. But if he inserts his arms or buttons it, it becomes prohibited. The ruling applies whether the pilgrim started wearing after ihram or was already wearing when entering ihram (continuous). It applies to the forgetful, ignorant, coerced, and even to a sleeper whose head is covered by someone else, because the benefit (tarafuq) still occurs; however, sin is removed due to lack of choice. It also applies whether one wears one garment or multiple garments (like shirt, turban, and socks) at once or separately—only one dam is required because all are considered one violation as long as they are done within one day. The author indicates that the ruling for more than one day is the same as for one day (i.e., one dam suffices for continuous days unless he removes with intention to stop).

Key Terms
لبس المخيط (labs al-makhīṭ): Wearing a sewn garment in the specific manner that involves enveloping the body (ishtimal) and adherence (istimsak) resulting from sewing.
تغطية الرأس (taghṭiyat al-ra's): Covering the head during ihram. If the covering is sewn, it is considered part of labs al-makhit and requires a dam. If unsewn, it is a minor violation requiring sadaqah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a pilgrim who covers his head with a sewn cap (like a 'araqiyya) during ihram?
A) Nothing is required because covering the head is not a violation.
B) He must offer a dam (blood sacrifice).
C) He must give sadaqah (charity).
D) He must perform a full Hajj again.

Key Takeaway
Covering the head with a sewn garment or wearing sewn garments that envelope the body requires a dam; unsewn coverings require only sadaqah.

Continuity of Wearing Sewn Garments in Ihram
Clarifying when continuous or intermittent wearing requires one or multiple penalties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when wearing a sewn garment continuously requires one dam and distinguish when multiple dams become necessary due to intention to permanently stop.

Core Idea
Continuity of wearing is broken only by a deliberate intention to permanently cease, not by mere removal.

Wearing sewn garments while in ihram is prohibited. But what if the pilgrim wears the same garment for several days, removing it only for sleep or to change? Does each day require a separate penalty? This lesson unravels the Hanafi position: the key is the wearer's intention at the moment of removal. A firm resolve to never wear it again triggers multiple penalties; otherwise, it is one continuous act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The act is wearing a sewn garment during ihram. The default ruling: one dam (blood sacrifice) is wajib if the wearing is continuous.
Continuous means the pilgrim wears it without a deliberate break.
2
Understand the role of intention at removal
If the pilgrim removes the garment with the firm intention (ʿazm) to never wear it again, then later wears it again, that is a new violation requiring another dam. If he removes it intending to resume (e.g., for sleep or to replace it with another sewn garment), the continuity is preserved and only one dam is needed.
The intention is evaluated at the moment of removal.
3
Analyze the internal Hanafi dispute
If the pilgrim expiates (offers a dam) for the first period and then continues wearing the same garment without removing it, Imam Muhammad holds that a new dam is required, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that no new dam is required as long as the wearing is continuous.
The majority view (mufta bihi) is that one dam suffices.
4
Apply the analogy from deposit law
The text cites a case from deposit law: a depositor who wears a deposited shirt without permission, removes it for sleep, and it is stolen – his liability depends on whether he intended to wear it again. This reinforces the principle that intention determines continuity.
This analogy shows the principle is consistent across fiqh.

فلو لبس المخيط ودام عليه أياماً أو كان ينزعه ليلاً ويعاوده نهاراً أو عكسه يلزمه دم واحد ما لم يعزم على الترك عند النزع، فإن عزم عليه ثم لبس تعدد الجزاء كفر للأول أولاً وفي الثاني خلاف محمد ولو لبس يوماً فأراق دماً ثم داوم على لبسه يوماً آخر كان عليه دم آخر بلا خلاف لأن للدوام فيه حكم الابتداء. وفي فقد حصل له في نسكه ارتفاق كامل فيكون تاركاً لواجب من واجبات إحرامه ، فينبغي أن يجب الدم؟ قلت : لا شك في نفاسته ولكن يحتاج إلى نقل صريح . ا هـ ملخصاً من حاشية المدني عن شرح المنسك للشيخ عبد الله العفيف، وفيها عن فتاوى تلميذه الفاضل عبد الله أفندي عتاقي أنه مال إلى وجوب الدم قوله: (والتحقيق أن تغطية الرأس الخ قال في النهر : التحقيق أن بين لبس المخيط والتغطية عموماً وخصوصاً مطلقاً فيجتمعان في التغطية في نحو العرقية المخيطة، وتنفرد التغطية بوضع نحو الشاش مما ليس مخيطاً على رأسه وهذا كاف في حصة التغاير قوله : (بواسطة الخياطة) يرد عليه اللباد المشتغل باللصق فإنه ليس فيه خياطة مع أنه عد من المخيط اللهم إلا أن يراد بالخياطة انضمام بعض الأجزاء ببعضها (شرح اللباب قوله : (أو جمع اللباس كله أي في مجلس واحد. كذا في شرح اللباب. ومفاده أنه لو اختلف المجلس في يوم واحد تعدد الجزاء وسنذكر عنه قريباً ما يخالفه قوله : (ما لم يعزم على الترك الخ) أي لم ينزعه على عزم الترك بل نزعه على قصد أن يلبسه ثانياً أو خلعه ليلبس بدله كذا في شرح اللباب. فقد أفاد أن خلعه لتبديله بغيره لا يتعدد به الجزاء فليحفظ فإنه كثير الوقوع قوله : (وفي الثاني) أي فيما إذا لم يكفر للأول قوله : ( وعندي المودع كذا في هذه النسخة بإضافة عند إلى ياء المتكلم وهكذا رأيته في  الفتاوى الظهيرية : وعندي المودع إذا لبس قميص الوديعة بغير إذن المودع فنزعه بالليل للنوم فسرق القميص في الليل فإن كان من قصده أن يلبس القميص من الغد لا يعد هذا ترك الخلاف والعود إلى الوفاق حتى يضمن وإن كان من قصده أن لا يلبس القميص من الغد كان هذا ترك الخلاف حتى لا يضمن. فالحاصل أن اللبس شيء واحد ما لم يتركه ويعزم على الترك اهـ .
So if he wears a sewn garment and continues upon it for days, or if he removes it at night and resumes it by day, or the opposite, one blood sacrifice is required of him as long as he does not intend to permanently stop at the time of removal. If he intends to stop and then wears [again], the penalty multiplies; if he expiated for the first [period] before wearing again, [there is ikhtilaf] — in the second [scenario] there is a difference from Muhammad. And if he wears for a day, slaughters a blood sacrifice, then continues upon wearing for another day, another blood sacrifice is upon him without dispute because in this case the continuance has the ruling of initiation. And concerning '[فقد حصل له]', it says: 'Indeed, full comfort has occurred for him in his rites, so he would be abandoning an obligatory act of his ihram, so a blood sacrifice should be obligatory?' I said: 'No doubt of its excellence, but it needs an explicit transmission.' Summarized from Hashiyat al-Madani from Sharh al-Mansak of Shaykh Abdullah al-ʿAfif, and in it from the fatwas of his excellent student Abdullah Afandi ʿAtaqi that he inclined to the obligation of the blood sacrifice. His statement: '(The verification that covering the head...)' — he said in al-Nahr: 'The verification is that between wearing sewn garments and covering the head there is general and specific in an absolute sense, so they combine in covering like in the case of a sewn ʿaraqiyya, and covering stands alone in placing something like [unsewn] muslin on his head, and this is sufficient for the aspect of difference.' His statement: '(By means of sewing)' — it is objected that felt (libad) which uses paste has no sewing in it, yet it is counted among sewn garments, unless by 'sewing' is meant the joining of some parts to one another (Sharh al-Lubab). His statement: '(Or gathers all garments)' — i.e., in one gathering (majlis). Thus in Sharh al-Lubab. And its implication is that if the gathering differs, even in one day, the penalty multiplies, and we will mention soon what contradicts it. His statement: '(As long as he does not intend to stop, etc.)' — i.e., he did not remove it with the intention of stopping, but removed it with the intention of wearing it again, or took it off to wear another in its place (Sharh al-Lubab). So it indicates that taking it off to replace it with another does not multiply the penalty, so remember it for it is frequently occurring. His statement: '(And in the second)' — i.e., when he did not expiate for the first. His statement: '(And with me the depositor)' — thus in this copy with the addition of ʿinda to the ya of the first person, and thus I saw it in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: 'And with me the depositor, if he wears a deposited shirt without the permission of the depositor, then removes it at night for sleep, and the shirt is stolen during the night — if his intention was to wear the shirt the next day, this is not considered cessation of violation and return to compliance, so he is liable. If his intention was not to wear the shirt the next day, this is considered cessation of violation and he is not liable.' So the conclusion is that wearing is one thing as long as he does not leave it and intend to stop. End.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion of the rule regarding continuity of wearing sewn garments during ihram. The author explains that if a pilgrim wears a sewn garment continuously for days—even if he removes it at night and puts it back in the morning, or vice versa—only one dam is required as long as at the time of removal he did not intend to permanently stop. If he does intend to stop and later wears again, then multiple dams are due. If he expiated for the first period (e.g., by offering a dam) before wearing again, there is disagreement: according to Imam Muhammad, the second wearing requires a new dam; according to the earlier position, one expiation suffices for the whole period. But if he wears, expiates, and then continues wearing (without removal), another dam is due by consensus, because the continuation after expiation is considered a new beginning. The text also quotes a discussion from Hashiyat al-Madani about whether a full day of comfort (tarafuq) necessitates a dam, but that requires an explicit text. The commentary then clarifies definitions: covering the head with a sewn item (like an ʿaraqiyya) is part of wearing sewn garments; with unsewn muslin it is not. 'Sewing' includes also pasted felt because the parts are joined. 'Gathering all garments' means in one session (majlis); if the sessions differ, penalties multiply. The intention when removing is crucial: if he removes to replace with another garment, it does not count as cessation. The text ends with an analogy from fiqh of deposits (al-wadiʿah): if a depositor wears a deposited shirt without permission then removes it for sleep, his liability for theft depends on his intention to wear again or not. This illustrates the principle that wearing is considered one continuous act unless one deliberately stops with the intention of not returning.

Key Terms
العزم على الترك (al-ʿazm ʿalā al-tark): The firm intention to permanently cease wearing a sewn garment. If present at the time of removal, it breaks the continuity and later wearing requires a new penalty.
المجلس (al-majlis): A session or gathering; if multiple sewn garments are worn in different sessions, even on the same day, multiple penalties are required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A pilgrim wears a sewn garment continuously for three days. He removes it each night for sleep but intends to wear it again in the morning. How many dam sacrifices are required?
A) Three dams, one for each day.
B) One dam, because the wearing is continuous without intention to permanently stop.
C) Two dams, one for the first day and one for the remaining two.
D) No dam, because he removed it at night.

Key Takeaway
Wearing a sewn garment continuously in ihram requires one dam if the wearer never intends to permanently stop; intention at removal is decisive.

Multiple Penalties for Wearing Sewn Garments: Causes and Exceptions
Enumerates when penalties multiply or unite for wearing sewn garments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the scenarios where penalties for wearing sewn garments multiply or unite, and apply the rules of cause (sabab) and session/day to determine the number of expiations required.

Core Idea
Penalties multiply when the cause multiplies; they unite if the cause is single and garments are worn in one session or day.

Wearing sewn garments while in ihram incurs a penalty (dam). However, the number of penalties depends on the underlying cause and the manner of wearing. This lesson unpacks the scenarios from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: when does a single act of wearing trigger multiple penalties, and when do multiple wearing acts merge into one? Understanding these principles ensures correct application of the expiation rules.

Guided Path
1
State the Basic Rule
Identify the default: one cause (sabab) for wearing sewn garments yields one penalty. The cause is the necessity (e.g., cold, wound).
The ruling assumes the pilgrim is not in a state of necessity that permits wearing without penalty.
2
Recognize Multiplicity of Cause
If the pilgrim has multiple distinct causes (e.g., cold and injury), or wears garments partly for necessity and partly without, penalties multiply accordingly.
Example: one garment on the wound (necessity) and another for warmth (luxury) → two expiations.
3
Learn the Five Cases of Multiplicity from al-Lubab
Memorize the five situations where a single wearing act incurs multiple penalties: (1) expiating between wearings, (2) multiple causes, (3) continuing after excuse ends, (4) a new excuse arising, (5) wearing a sewn garment dyed with perfume (for men).
These are listed in the Tanbeeh quote.
4
Understand Unifying Factors
Even if multiple garments are worn, penalties unite if: the cause is single, there is no intention to permanently discontinue wearing upon removal, and all garments are worn in one session (majlis) or one day (yawm).
The session/day distinction is debated within the school.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Day vs. Session
Some authorities (like al-Farsi and al-Tarabulsi) restrict unity to one day; others allow unity by session. Munyat al-Nasik explicitly makes penalties multiply across different days.
The mufta bihi follows multiplication across days; within one day or session, one penalty suffices if cause is single.

Know that what we have mentioned of obligating the penalty when he wears all sewn garments applies when the cause of wearing does not multiply. If it multiplies, as when he is compelled to wear a garment and he wears two garments: if he wears both on the place of necessity, he owes one expiation, and he may choose [between options] in it; if he wears them on the place of necessity and elsewhere, two expiations are required, and he may choose only for the necessity part. His statement: (So the conclusion, etc.) He said in al-Lubab: (A note): The penalty may multiply in one wearing due to matters: first, expiation between two wearings, i.e., he wore, then expiated, and continued upon his wearing and did not remove it. Second, multiplicity of cause. Third, continuance upon wearing after the excuse ceases. Fourth, occurrence of another excuse. Fifth, wearing a sewn garment dyed with perfume for a man. And the penalty may become single despite multiplicity of wearing due to matters: unity of cause, absence of intention to permanently stop at removal, and gathering all garments in one session or one day. In al-Kirmani: and if he gathers all garments in one day because it occurs in one way and one cause, it becomes like a single violation. Similarly what some mentioned concerning shaving the head: if he shaves in four sessions, one dam is upon him, and it was said four dams. And it is explicitly stated in Munyat al-Nasik that the penalty multiplies with multiplication of days, where he said: if he wears a turban one day, then a shirt another day, then socks another day, then trousers another day, for each wearing a dam is upon him. His statement: (If he wears both on the place of necessity, one expiation is upon him; likewise if he wears them on two places out of necessity for both in one session, as when he wears a turban and socks due to an excuse in both, one expiation is upon him, and it is the expiation of necessity because the wearing is in one way, so one expiation is owed. Thus in Sharh al-Lubab. His statement: (And from the scenarios of multiplicity of wearing) — thus in the copies I have seen, and what is in al-Fath and al-Nahr is 'multiplicity of cause' instead of 'wearing'. Then he narrated from al-Fath the issue of fever in his rites.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive enumeration from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq of the scenarios where penalties for wearing sewn garments multiply or unite. The main principle is that the ruling of one penalty applies when the cause (sabab) is single. If the cause multiplies, penalties multiply. Examples include necessity: if a pilgrim is compelled to wear sewn garments due to necessity, and he wears two garments where both are necessary, he owes one expiation (he may choose between sacrifice, fasting, or feeding the poor). If one garment is necessary and the other is not, he owes two expiations, but can only choose for the necessary one. The text then quotes a 'Tanbeeh' from al-Lubab listing five cases where one wearing act requires multiple penalties: (1) expiating between two wearing periods (e.g., he wears, expiates, then continues without removing), (2) multiple causes (e.g., cold and covering a wound), (3) continuing to wear after the excuse ends, (4) a new excuse arising, (5) wearing a sewn garment dyed with perfume (for a man). Conversely, multiple wearing acts unite into one penalty if: the cause is one, there is no intention to permanently stop when removing, and all garments are worn in one session (majlis) or one day (yawm). The commentary notes a difference: some authorities restrict unity by the day (like al-Farsi and al-Tarabulsi), while others consider the session. For example, wearing different garments on different days (turban on day 1, shirt on day 2, etc.) requires a separate dam for each day, as stated in Munyat al-Nasik. The text also clarifies that if necessity arises in two places (e.g., head and feet) but in one session, one expiation suffices because the cause is one. Finally, it warns that the author (al-Kanz) will later mention a contradictory ruling regarding perfume and wearing, so the reader should be alert.

Key Terms
سبب اللبس (sabab al-labs): The cause that necessitates wearing sewn garments (e.g., cold, necessity). If the cause is single, one penalty; if multiple (e.g., cold and wound), penalties multiply.
اليوم (al-yawm): The day (daytime) within which wearing multiple sewn garments may be counted as one violation if done in one session or with one cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Lubab, which of the following is NOT one of the factors that cause a single wearing act to require multiple penalties?
A) Expiation between wearings (wearing, expiating, then continuing without removing).
B) Multiple distinct causes (e.g., cold and wound).
C) Wearing the garments in separate sessions on the same day.
D) Wearing a sewn garment dyed with perfume (for a man).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the number of penalties for wearing sewn garments during ihram is determined by the cause (sabab): one cause = one penalty, multiple causes = multiple penalties, with additional factors such as continuation after excuse and perfumed garments also multiplying penalties.

Multiplicity of Expiation for Sewn Garments in Ihram
Commentary expanding on when multiple expiations are due for wearing sewn garments in ihram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for multiplicity of expiation when a muhrim wears multiple sewn garments, distinguishing between a single cause and multiple causes.

Core Idea
One necessity, one expiation — multiple necessities, multiple expiations.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle point in Hajj penalties: when wearing several sewn garments in ihram, how many expiations does the pilgrim owe? The answer hinges on the cause (sabab), not the number of garments. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the rules correctly in complex scenarios like layering for cold or fashion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying principle
The ruling depends on the unity or multiplicity of the cause (sabab) for wearing sewn garments, not the number of garments. Determine the reason for each garment worn.
The passage emphasizes 'cause' over 'act'.
2
Apply to a basic case
If a muhrim is forced by necessity (darurah) to wear clothing, wearing two garments for that same necessity incurs only one expiation.
Example: two shirts for extreme cold.
3
Distinguish mixed causes
If the wearing covers both necessity and non-necessity (e.g., comfort or fashion), two expiations are due — one for the necessity portion and one for the non-necessity.
Example: one shirt for cold, one jacket for style.
4
Consider the time dimension
Gathering all garments in one day or one sitting with a single cause counts as one wearing; spreading over multiple days with separate intentions multiplies expiations.
The day vs. sitting distinction is mentioned but not definitive; multiple days usually mean multiple expiations.
5
Note the relied-upon position
The mufta bihi is that one expiation is due for a single necessity even with multiple garments, and two expiations when the wearing includes both necessity and non-necessity.
This is established in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الفتاوى الظهيرية : وعندي المودع إذا لبس قميص الوديعة بغير إذن المودع فنزعه بالليل للنوم فسرق القميص في الليل فإن كان من قصده أن يلبس القميص من الغد لا يعد هذا ترك الخلاف والعود إلى الوفاق حتى يضمن وإن كان من قصده أن لا يلبس القميص من الغد كان هذا ترك الخلاف حتى لا يضمن. فالحاصل أن اللبس شيء واحد ما لم يتركه ويعزم على الترك اهـ.

واعلم أن ما ذكرناه من إيجاب الجزاء إذا لبس جميع المخيط محله ما إذا لم يتعدد سبب اللبس فإن تعدد كما إذا اضطر إلى لبس ثوب فلبس ثوبين فإن لبسهما على موضع الضرورة فعليه كفارة واحدة يتخير فيها، وإن لبسهما على موضع الضرورة وغيره لزمه كفارتان يتخير فيما للضرورة فقط . ومن صور تعدد اللبس واتحاده ما إذا كان به مثلاً يحتاج إلى اللبس واحد فعليه دم همي الظهيرية وفي سائر النسخ بدون ياء قوله : ( فالحاصل الخ ) قال في اللباب: (تنبيه): قد يتعدد الجزاء في لبس واحد بأمور : الأول التكفير بين اللبسين بأن لبس ثم كفر ودام على لبسه ولم ينزعه . والثاني تعدد السبب. والثالث الاستمرار على اللبس بعد زوال العذر والرابع حدوث عذر آخر. والخامس لبس المخيط المصبوغ بطيب للرجل. ويتحد الجزاء تعدد اللبس بأمور منها : اتحاد السبب وعدم العزم على الترك عند النزع وجمع اللباس كله في مجلس أو يوم ا ا هـ. قال شارحه : أي . مع اتحاد السبب ثم قال : واعلم أنه ذكر بعضهم ما يفيد أن اليوم في اتحاد الجزاء في حكم اللباس كالمجلس في غيره من الطيب والحلق والقص والجماع كما سيأتي لأنه ذكر الفارسي والطرابلسي أنه إن لبس الثياب كلها معاً ولبس خفين فعليه دم واحد وإن لبس قميصاً بعد يومه ثم لبس في يومه سراويل ثم لبس خفين وقلنسوة عليه كفارة واحدة فقيد باليوم لا بالمجلس. وفي الكرماني ولو جمع اللباس كله في يوم واحد لوقوعه على جهة واحدة وسبب واحد فصار كجناية واحدة، ومثله ما ذكره بعضهم في حلق الرأس إذا حلق في أربكة مجالس عليه دم واحد وقيل عليه أربع دماء وقد صرح في منية الناسك بتعدد الجزاء في تعدد الأيام حيث قال : وإن لبس العمامة يوماً ثم لبس Book of Hajj, Chapter of Penalties. Al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: According to me, if a depositee wears a borrowed shirt without the depositor's permission, then removes it at night to sleep, and it is stolen at night, then if he intended to wear it again the next day, this is not considered abandonment of disagreement and return to agreement, so he is liable. But if he intended not to wear it again, this is considered abandonment of disagreement, so he is not liable. The conclusion is that wearing is one act as long as he does not abandon it and resolve to abandon it. End. Know that what we mentioned regarding the obligation of expiation when wearing all sewn garments applies when the cause of wearing is not multiple. If it is multiple, such as when he is forced to wear a garment and wears two garments, then if he wears both for the place of necessity, he owes one expiation with choice; if he wears them for necessity and other, he owes two expiations with choice only for the necessity. Among the forms of multiplicity and unity of wearing: if he has, for example, a need for one wearing, he owes one expiation. 'Al-Zahiriyya' (with ya in some copies). His statement: (so the conclusion) etc. He said in al-Lubab: (Note) Expiation may multiply in a single wearing due to several matters: first, expiating between two wearings, such as wearing then expiating and continuing wearing without removal; second, multiplicity of cause; third, continuing to wear after excuse ceases; fourth, occurrence of another excuse; fifth, wearing sewn garment dyed with perfume for a man. And expiation is unified despite multiplicity of wearing due to matters including: unity of cause, lack of resolve to abandon at removal, gathering all garments in one sitting or one day. End. Its commentator said: i.e., with unity of cause. Then he said: Know that some mentioned that the day in unification of expiation in the ruling of clothing is like the sitting in other matters of perfume, shaving, cutting, and intercourse, as will come. Because he mentioned al-Farsi and al-Tarabulsi that if he wears all garments together and two socks, he owes one expiation; if he wears a shirt one day, then pants later, then socks and cap, he owes one expiation, qualified by day, not sitting. In al-Kirmani: If he gathers all clothing in one day, occurring in one manner and one cause, it becomes like one offense. Similarly, some said in shaving the head: if he shaves in multiple sittings, he owes one expiation, and it was said four expiations. It is explicitly stated in Munyat al-Nasik: expiation multiplies with multiple days; if he wears a turban one day, then a shirt another day, then socks another day, then pants another day, he owes an expiation for each wearing. End. His statement: (as when forced to wear a garment and wears two garments) He said in al-Fath: similarly, if he is forced to wear a shirt and wears two shirts, or a shirt and a jubba, or forced to wear a cap and wears it with a turban. End. Similarly in al-Mi'raj and Ghayat al-Bayan. We only mentioned this because the author will mention what contradicts it at his statement 'and if he applies perfume or wears' so be aware. His statement: (if he wears both for the place of necessity, he owes one expiation; similarly if he wears both for two places of necessity in one sitting, such as wearing a turban and socks due to an excuse in both, he owes one expiation, which is the expiation of necessity, because the wearing is in one manner, so one expiation is obligatory, as in Sharh al-Lubab. His statement: (among the forms of multiplicity of wearing) is how it appears in the copies I have seen, but in al-Fath and al-Nahr it is 'cause' instead of 'wearing'. His statement: (and al-Halabi mentioned that its implication etc.) He said in al-Nahr: the ruling in the madhhab is recorded similarly, then he narrated from al-Fath the issue of fever.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rules of expiation (kaffarah) for a pilgrim in ihram when wearing sewn garments (makhit), focusing on when multiple expiations are due. The text is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq and expands on the distinction between unity and multiplicity of the cause (sabab) of wearing. The author first cites a ruling from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya about a depositee wearing a borrowed garment, establishing that a single wearing continues as long as one intends to wear again. This principle is then applied to the pilgrim's wearing of sewn garments: if the necessity (darurah) that justifies wearing is single, even if multiple garments are worn, only one expiation is due; if the wearing covers both necessity and non-necessity, two expiations are due. The commentator elaborates with examples from various sources: wearing two shirts for one necessity requires one expiation; wearing a shirt and a jubba for both necessity and comfort requires two. The length of time (one day vs. multiple days) also matters: wearing multiple garments spread over multiple days incurs multiple expiations according to Munyat al-Nasik. The passage emphasizes that the key factor is the unity or multiplicity of the cause, not the physical act of wearing itself.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for violating ihram prohibitions, specifically for wearing sewn garments.
مخيط (makhit): Sewn garment; any clothing tailored to fit the body, forbidden for the muhrim.
سبب (sabab): Cause or reason for wearing, such as necessity (darurah) or comfort.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muhrim wears a sewn cap for sun protection (necessity) and a sewn coat for fashion. How many expiations are due?
A) One
B) Two
C) None, because necessity covers both
D) One for the cap, none for the coat

Key Takeaway
The multiplicity of expiation depends on the cause (sabab), not the number of garments; a single cause yields one expiation even with many garments, while multiple causes multiply the expiation.

Multiple Expiations for Sewn Garments: The Criterion of Jihah
Rules for multiple expiations when necessity ceases and returns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether one or multiple expiations are required when a muhrim repeatedly wears sewn garments due to necessity by applying the Hanafi criterion of jihah (unity or multiplicity of the underlying cause).

Core Idea
The number of expiations depends on the unity or multiplicity of the necessity's aspect (jihah), not the frequency of wearing.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a muhrim who wears sewn garments due to necessity must offer an expiation (dam). But what if he wears them multiple times over several days? Does every removal and re-wearing require a new expiation? In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author clarifies that the key is continuity of the underlying cause, not the physical acts. We will analyze the rule for when necessity ends and a new one begins, and understand the intra-Hanafi difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad on this point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage describes two main scenarios: (a) continuous necessity (same fever, same enemy) vs (b) separate necessities (different fever, different enemy). Make a list of these from the text.
Note that the same necessity can span multiple days of wearing.
2
Extract the legal principle
From the text: 'والأصل في جنس هذه المسائل أنه ينظر إلى اتحاد الجهة واختلافها'. Understand that jihah is the aspect or cause that makes the wearing necessary.
Jihah is not the act of wearing itself but the reason behind it.
3
Apply the principle to the fever example
If a person has a fever, wears sewn garments, then recovers and later gets a different fever, the jihah is different. According to Abu Hanifa, two expiations are due. Compare with Muhammad's view.
The text says 'خلافاً لمحمد في الثاني', meaning Muhammad holds only one expiation for the second fever.
4
Apply the principle to the enemy example
Similarly, if one enemy siege ends and a new, different enemy appears, two expiations are due. The continuity of the same enemy requires only one expiation.
The principle of jihah applies uniformly.
5
Consider doubt and certainty
If there is doubt about whether the necessity has ceased, only one expiation is due. Certainty of cessation triggers a new expiation.
This shows that the burden of proof lies with certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زوال لها ويستغنى عنه في وقت زوالها فإن عليه كفارة واحدة وإن تعدد اللبس ما لم تزل عنه، فإن زالت وأصابه مرض آخر أو حمى غيرها فعليه كفارتان كفر للأولى أولاً خلافاً لمحمد في الثاني. وكذا إذا حصره عدوّ فاحتاج إلى اللبس للقتال أياماً يلبسها إذا خرج إليه وينزعها إذا فعليه كفارة واحدة ما لم يذهب هذا العدوّ ، فإن ذهب وجاء عدوّ غيره لزمه كفارة رجع أخرى. والأصل في جنس هذه المسائل أنه ينظر إلى اتحاد الجهة واختلافها لا إلى صورة اللبس كيف كانت ولو لبس لضرورة فزالت فدام بعدها يوماً ويومين فما دام في شك من الضرورة فليس عليه إلا كفارة ،واحدة وإن تيقن زوالها كان عليه كفارة أخرى لا يتخير فيها. هكذا ذكروا . وذكر الحلبي في مناسكه أن مقتضاه أنه إذا لبس شيئاً من المخيط لدفع برد ثم صار ينزع ويلبس كذلك ثم زال ذلك البرد ثم أصابه برد آخر غير الأول عرف ذلك بوجه من الوجوه المفيدة لمعرفته فلبس لذلك أنه يجب عليه كفارتان .اهـ. وشمل كلامه أيضاً ما إذا لم يجد غير المخيط فلذا قال في المجمع : ولو لم يجد إلا السراويل فلبسه ولم يفتقه نوجبه أي الدم.
If the excuse ceases and he dispenses with it at the time of its cessation, then he owes one expiation even if the wearing is multiple as long as it has not ceased. If it ceases and he is struck by another illness or a different fever, he owes two expiations, expiating for the first first, contrary to Muhammad regarding the second. Similarly, if an enemy besieges him and he needs to wear for fighting for days, wearing when he goes out and removing when he returns, he owes one expiation as long as this enemy does not go away. If it goes and another enemy comes, he owes another expiation upon return. The principle in these matters is that one looks at the unity or difference of the aspect (jihah), not the form of wearing however it may be. If he wears due to necessity and it ceases, and he continues after it for a day or two, as long as he is in doubt about the necessity, he owes only one expiation. If he is certain of its cessation, he owes another expiation without choice. Thus they mentioned. Al-Halabi mentioned in his Manasik that its implication is that if he wears something sewn to repel cold, then he removes and wears similarly, then that cold ceases and he is struck by another cold not the first, known by some means that befits its knowledge, then wears for it, he owes two expiations. End. And his statement also includes the case when he finds no sewn garment other than it. Hence it is said in al-Majma': If he finds only trousers, wears them without ripping, we obligate the blood (i.e., expiation).
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on expiations for wearing sewn garments, focusing on scenarios where the necessity (such as illness or enemy threat) ends and then a new necessity arises. The key principle is that a single necessity, even if prolonged over multiple days, requires only one expiation. However, if the necessity completely ceases and then a new, separate necessity occurs, a second expiation becomes obligatory. For example, if a pilgrim has a fever, wears sewn garments, and after recovery is struck by a different fever, two expiations are due according to Abu Hanifa (contrary to Muhammad who holds that the second does not require a separate expiation). Similarly, if an enemy siege ends and a different enemy attacks, a second expiation is due. The text emphasizes that the determining factor is the unity or multiplicity of the 'aspect' (jihah), meaning the underlying cause, not the number of times sewn garments are physically put on or taken off. If there is doubt about the cessation of necessity, only one expiation is required; certainty of cessation triggers another. The passage also notes a ruling from al-Majma': if only trousers are available and the pilgrim wears them without ripping them (to make them permissible), he owes the expiation (blood). This underscores that necessity does not remove the obligation of expiation; it only affects the number.

Key Terms
جهة (jihah): Aspect or underlying cause of the necessity; unity or multiplicity of jihah determines number of expiations.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that permits wearing sewn garments in ihram, such as extreme cold, illness, or enemy threat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muhrim wears sewn garments due to a severe cold for two days. The cold disappears, and two days later he develops a fever and wears again. How many expiations does he owe according to the mufta bihi?
A) One expiation, because both are medical necessities.
B) Two expiations, because the cold and fever are separate necessities.
C) Three expiations, because there are three days of wearing.
D) No expiation, because he was ill.

Key Takeaway
The number of expiations for wearing sewn garments in ihram is determined by the unity or multiplicity of the necessity (jihah), not the count of wearing events.

Covering with Non-Customary Items: No Penalty
Definition of covering that incurs penalty for a muhrim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a muhrim covering his head or face with an item not customarily used for covering, and apply it to examples like the Kaaba curtain.

Core Idea
A basin on the head incurs no penalty; a turban does.

The prohibition on covering the head and face during ihram targets intentional use of items normally worn as headgear. This lesson clarifies that objects like bowls or baskets, which are not customary coverings, do not trigger an expiation, even if they touch the head. We also examine the case of a muhrim under the Kaaba's covering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a muhrim covering his head/face with an object. The text distinguishes between two types of objects.
2
Define customary covering
Customary covering means items normally used for head/face covering like a cap or turban.
Urf (custom) is the basis.
3
Apply to abnormal items
Items like a basin (tast) are not customary; covering with them incurs no penalty.
Text says 'nothing is due on him'.
4
Consider the Kaaba curtain case
The text uses this as an example: entering under the Kaaba's curtain and it touching head/face is makruh but no penalty.
Makruh tanzihi, no dam.
5
Connect to the broader ruling
This distinction sets the scope for the subsequent dispute on the amount of coverage required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في التغطية فانصرفت إلى الكامل وهو ما يغطى به عادة كالقلنسوة والعمامة فخرج ما لا يغطى به عادة كالطست والإجانة والعدل فلا شيء عليه وعلى هذا يفرع ما في الظهيرية : ما لو دخل المحرم تحت ستر الكعبة وإن كان يصيب وجهه ورأسه فهو مكروه لا شيء عليه وإلا فلا بأس به، وظاهر ما في المتون يقتضي أنه لا بد من تغطية جميع المأس في لزوم الدم، وما رأيته رواية ولهذا لم يصرحوا بحكم ما دونها وإنما المنقول عن الأصل اعتبار الربع ومشى عليه كثير واختاره في الظهيرية مقتصراً عليه وعزاه في الهداية إلى أنه عن أبي حنيفة، وعن محمد اعتبار الأكثر وهو مروي عن أبي يوسف أيضاً كما اعتبر أكثر اليوم في لزوم الدم، واختاره في فتح القدير من جهة الدراية. فالحاصل أن الربع راجح رواية والأكثر السابقة قوله : (وما رأيته (رواية أي ما رأيت ظاهر ما في المتون مروياً، وقوله «ولهذا» علة لقوله يقتضى» لا لقوله وما رأيته والضمير في «لم يصرحوا لأصحاب المتون. وفي شرح اللباب : واعلم أنه إذا استر بعض كل منهما أي الوجه والرأس. فالمشهور من الرواية عن أبي حنيفة أنه اعتبر الربع فبتغطية ربع الرأس يجب ما يجب بكله كما ذكر في غير موضع وهو الصحيح على ما قاله غير واحد. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يعتبر أكثر الرأس على ما نقل عنه صاحب الهداية والكافي والمبسوط ونقله في المحيط والذخيرة والبدائع والكرماني عن محمد لكن قال الزيلعي : وقياس قول محمد أن يعتبر فيه الوجوب بحسابه من الدم اهـ. وكذا الحكم في الوجه على ما نص عليه في المبسوط والوجيز وغيرهما. وأما ما في خزانة الأكمل وإن غطى ثلث رأسه أو شيء عليه بخلاف الحلق فهو شاذ مخالف لكلام غيره بل لكلامه أيضاً فإنه قال في موضع آخر وبتغطية ربع وجهه أو ربع رأسه يجب ما يجب بكله اللهم إلا أن يقال : أراد بقوله لا شيء عليه أي من الدم لا من ) ويكون بناء على قولهما لا على قول الإمام الأعظم والله أعلم ا هـ قوله : (فأفاد أن الليلة كاليوم)
And he made absolute (the ruling) regarding covering; it refers to the complete covering, which is what is customarily used for covering like a cap and turban. Thus excluded from it is what is not customarily used for covering like a basin (tast), a washbasin (ijanah), and a saddlebag (adl). So nothing is due on him. On this is based what is in al-Zahiriyya: if a muhrim enters under the covering of the Kaaba, even if it touches his face and head, it is makruh but nothing is due on him; otherwise there is no harm. And the apparent meaning of what is in the texts (mutun) requires that covering of all the (body) part is necessary for the obligation of blood. But I have not seen this as a narration, and for this they did not explicitly state the ruling for what is less than that. What is transmitted from the original is the consideration of the quarter (rub'), and many followed it, and it is chosen in al-Zahiriyya confining to it, and attributed in al-Hidayah to Abu Hanifa. From Muhammad, the consideration of the majority, and it is also narrated from Abu Yusuf, just as he considered the majority of the day for the obligation of blood. And it is chosen in Fath al-Qadir from the aspect of reasoning. So the conclusion is that the quarter is preponderant in narration, and the majority (is the other). His statement: (and I have not seen it as a narration) meaning I have not seen the apparent meaning of the texts as a narration. And his statement 'and for this' is the cause for his saying 'requires', not for his saying 'and I have not seen it'. In Sharh al-Lubab: Know that if he covers part of both, i.e., the face and head, then the well-known narration from Abu Hanifa is that he considered the quarter; so covering a quarter of the head necessitates what is necessitated by covering all, as mentioned in other places, and it is the correct view according to many. From Abu Yusuf, he considers the majority of the head, as transmitted from him by the author of al-Hidayah, al-Kafi, and al-Mabsut, and it is transmitted in al-Muhit, al-Dhakhira, al-Bada'i, and al-Kirmani from Muhammad. But al-Zayla'i said: The analogical deduction from Muhammad's statement is that obligation is considered proportionally from the blood. End. Similarly, the ruling for the face as stated in al-Mabsut, al-Wajiz, and others. As for what is in Khizanat al-Akmal: if he covers a third of his head or something similarly, unlike shaving, it is anomalous, contradicting others' statements, even his own, for he said elsewhere: covering a quarter of his face or a quarter of his head necessitates what is necessitated by covering all, unless it is said that he meant by 'nothing is due on him' nothing due of blood, not of (something else), and it is based on their (the two companions') statement, not on that of the Supreme Imam (Abu Hanifa). And Allah knows best. End. His statement: (so it indicates that the night is like the day).
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two issues: first, what kinds of covering incur the penalty (dam) for a muhrim covering his head/face, and second, what amount of coverage triggers that penalty. The author explains that the prohibition applies only to items customarily used for covering, such as a cap or turban. Items not normally used for covering, like a basin or basket, do not incur a penalty even if they touch the head. This is applied to the case of a muhrim standing under the covering of the Kaaba: if it touches his face and head, it is makruh but no expiation is due. Then the text turns to the amount of coverage needed to obligate the penalty. The apparent meaning of earlier texts suggested that covering the entire head is required, but the author notes that this is not a strong narration. The original position is from Abu Hanifa: covering a quarter of the head incurs the full penalty. This is the preponderant narration. From Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the threshold is covering the majority of the head. Some later sources like Fath al-Qadir prefer the majority view by reasoning, but the quarter view is stronger by transmission. The same rule applies to the face. A minority view that a third suffices is rejected as anomalous. The passage concludes by noting that the night is treated like the day in these rulings.

Key Terms
ربع (rub'): One quarter of the head or face; the minimum coverage that obligates full expiation according to Abu Hanifa.
أكثر (akthar): Majority (more than half) of the head or face; the threshold accepted by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
تغطية (taghtiyah): Covering of the head or face by an object; the ruling depends on whether the object is customarily used for covering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muhrim entering under the Kaaba's curtain that touches his head is:
A) Haram with penalty
B) Makruh with no penalty
C) Mubah
D) Wajib to remove

Key Takeaway
Covering the head or face with a non-customary item incurs no penalty; only items normally used for covering trigger the prohibition.

The Quarter Threshold: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Amount of Coverage
Dispute among Hanafi imams on the amount of head/face coverage triggering expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the threshold of head/face coverage that obligates expiation, and identify the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Covering a quarter obligates expiation; covering less is excused.

Even when a muhrim uses a customary covering, the question remains: how much coverage triggers the penalty? Abu Hanifa held that covering a quarter suffices, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad required covering the majority. This lesson unpacks their proofs and the preferred fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
The issue is the minimum amount of head/face coverage that makes dam obligatory on a muhrim.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa: covering one quarter (rub') incurs the full penalty. This is the well-known narration.
Quarter = 25% of the area.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: covering the majority (akthar, more than half) is the threshold.
Thus covering half may not obligate penalty under their view.
4
Analyze the proof (riwayah vs dirayah)
Abu Hanifa's view is stronger by transmission (riwayah); the Sahibayn's view relies on analogical reasoning (dirayah).
Tarjih: riwayah overrides dirayah in this case.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The quarter view is the relied-upon fatwa due to its stronger narration in the Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is the preferred view for practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And he made absolute (the ruling) regarding covering; it refers to the complete covering, which is what is customarily used for covering like a cap and turban. Thus excluded from it is what is not customarily used for covering like a basin (tast), a washbasin (ijanah), and a saddlebag (adl). So nothing is due on him. On this is based what is in al-Zahiriyya: if a muhrim enters under the covering of the Kaaba, even if it touches his face and head, it is makruh but nothing is due on him; otherwise there is no harm. And the apparent meaning of what is in the texts (mutun) requires that covering of all the (body) part is necessary for the obligation of blood. But I have not seen this as a narration, and for this they did not explicitly state the ruling for what is less than that. What is transmitted from the original is the consideration of the quarter (rub'), and many followed it, and it is chosen in al-Zahiriyya confining to it, and attributed in al-Hidayah to Abu Hanifa. From Muhammad, the consideration of the majority, and it is also narrated from Abu Yusuf, just as he considered the majority of the day for the obligation of blood. And it is chosen in Fath al-Qadir from the aspect of reasoning. So the conclusion is that the quarter is preponderant in narration, and the majority (is the other). His statement: (and I have not seen it as a narration) meaning I have not seen the apparent meaning of the texts as a narration. And his statement 'and for this' is the cause for his saying 'requires', not for his saying 'and I have not seen it'. In Sharh al-Lubab: Know that if he covers part of both, i.e., the face and head, then the well-known narration from Abu Hanifa is that he considered the quarter; so covering a quarter of the head necessitates what is necessitated by covering all, as mentioned in other places, and it is the correct view according to many. From Abu Yusuf, he considers the majority of the head, as transmitted from him by the author of al-Hidayah, al-Kafi, and al-Mabsut, and it is transmitted in al-Muhit, al-Dhakhira, al-Bada'i, and al-Kirmani from Muhammad. But al-Zayla'i said: The analogical deduction from Muhammad's statement is that obligation is considered proportionally from the blood. End. Similarly, the ruling for the face as stated in al-Mabsut, al-Wajiz, and others. As for what is in Khizanat al-Akmal: if he covers a third of his head or something similarly, unlike shaving, it is anomalous, contradicting others' statements, even his own, for he said elsewhere: covering a quarter of his face or a quarter of his head necessitates what is necessitated by covering all, unless it is said that he meant by 'nothing is due on him' nothing due of blood, not of (something else), and it is based on their (the two companions') statement, not on that of the Supreme Imam (Abu Hanifa). And Allah knows best. End. His statement: (so it indicates that the night is like the day).
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two issues: first, what kinds of covering incur the penalty (dam) for a muhrim covering his head/face, and second, what amount of coverage triggers that penalty. The author explains that the prohibition applies only to items customarily used for covering, such as a cap or turban. Items not normally used for covering, like a basin or basket, do not incur a penalty even if they touch the head. This is applied to the case of a muhrim standing under the covering of the Kaaba: if it touches his face and head, it is makruh but no expiation is due. Then the text turns to the amount of coverage needed to obligate the penalty. The apparent meaning of earlier texts suggested that covering the entire head is required, but the author notes that this is not a strong narration. The original position is from Abu Hanifa: covering a quarter of the head incurs the full penalty. This is the preponderant narration. From Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the threshold is covering the majority of the head. Some later sources like Fath al-Qadir prefer the majority view by reasoning, but the quarter view is stronger by transmission. The same rule applies to the face. A minority view that a third suffices is rejected as anomalous. The passage concludes by noting that the night is treated like the day in these rulings.

Key Terms
ربع (rub'): One quarter of the head or face; the minimum coverage that obligates full expiation according to Abu Hanifa.
أكثر (akthar): Majority (more than half) of the head or face; the threshold accepted by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
تغطية (taghtiyah): Covering of the head or face by an object; the ruling depends on whether the object is customarily used for covering.

C) Half (50%)
D) Majority (more than 50%)

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi fatwa is that covering a quarter of the head or face with a customary item obligates the full expiation.

Multiple Violations in Ihram: Unity of Cause
Principle of unity of cause for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ittiḥād al-jihah to determine the number of expiations required for multiple ihram violations.

Core Idea
Expiation depends on the cause's unity, not the number of acts.

In this passage, the commentator explains a crucial Hanafi principle for determining expiation (dam) when a muhrim commits multiple violations like wearing stitched clothes. The key is to look at the cause: if the need (such as illness or enemy threat) continues, all similar acts are treated as one. But if the cause changes, each new cause demands a separate expiation. This principle also resolves an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage concerns a muhrim wearing stitched clothes (makhit) due to necessity. This is a violation (janiyah) requiring an expiation (kaffarah, specifically a dam).
The ruling is that the expiation is wajib.
2
Recognize the key principle
The passage states: 'The principle in this type of issue is that one considers the unity or difference of the cause, not the form of wearing.' This is ittiḥād al-jihah (unity of cause).
The cause (jihah) is the underlying need, e.g., illness or enemy threat.
3
Apply to the illness scenario
If a muhrim wears due to one illness, then it ends and a new illness begins, Abu Hanifa says two dams are required (one for each illness), while Muhammad says one dam if no gap. This shows how the principle is applied differently.
The disagreement turns on whether the cessation of the first illness breaks the unity of cause.
4
Apply to the enemy scenario
If a muhrim wears due to one enemy, then that enemy leaves and a new enemy arrives, two dams are required. The cause changed, so unity is broken.
This example illustrates the default rule: distinct causes = multiple expiations.
5
Handle uncertainty
If the muhrim is uncertain whether the need has ended, he owes only one dam. Certainty of the end triggers a second dam.
This is a practical application: doubt favors the continuity of the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زوال لها ويستغنى عنه في وقت زوالها فإن عليه كفارة واحدة وإن تعدد اللبس ما لم تزل عنه، فإن زالت وأصابه مرض آخر أو حمى غيرها فعليه كفارتان كفر للأولى أولاً خلافاً لمحمد في الثاني. وكذا إذا حصره عدوّ فاحتاج إلى اللبس للقتال أياماً يلبسها إذا خرج إليه وينزعها إذا فعليه كفارة واحدة ما لم يذهب هذا العدوّ ، فإن ذهب وجاء عدوّ غيره لزمه كفارة رجع أخرى. والأصل في جنس هذه المسائل أنه ينظر إلى اتحاد الجهة واختلافها لا إلى صورة اللبس كيف كانت ولو لبس لضرورة فزالت فدام بعدها يوماً ويومين فما دام في شك من الضرورة فليس عليه إلا كفارة ،واحدة وإن تيقن زوالها كان عليه كفارة أخرى لا يتخير فيها. هكذا ذكروا
The end of [the need] and he is dispensed with at the time of its end, then upon him is one expiation even if the wearing is repeated as long as it has not ended from him. If it ends and another illness or different fever afflicts him, then upon him are two expiations: an expiation for the first first, contrary to Muhammad regarding the second. Similarly, if an enemy besieges him and he needs to wear [stitched clothes] for fighting for days, he wears them when he goes out to him and removes them when [he returns], then upon him is one expiation as long as this enemy has not left. If he leaves and another enemy comes, he is obligated for another expiation in return. The principle in this type of issue is that one considers the unity or difference of the cause, not the form of wearing. And if he wears out of necessity, then it ends, and he continues after it for a day or two, then as long as he is in doubt about the necessity, he owes only one expiation. If he is certain of its end, he owes another expiation without choice. Thus they mentioned.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the ruling on multiple violations (jinnayat) during ihram, specifically regarding wearing stitched clothes (makhit). The key principle is that the unity or multiplicity of the cause (al-jihah) determines the number of expiations (kaffarat), not the number of times the act is performed. If the same need (like illness or enemy threat) persists, even with repeated wearing, only one dam is required. If the need ends and a new need arises, a second dam becomes due. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) differs in one detail: when a second illness follows the first, Abu Hanifa requires two dams (for each illness), while Muhammad considers it one if no gap occurred. The passage also clarifies that uncertainty about the necessity's end keeps the initial kaffarah valid; only certainty of its end requires a new one. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, based on the principle of considering the cause's continuity.

Key Terms
اتحاد الجهة (ittiḥād al-jihah): Unity of the cause or reason for the concession; if the same need persists, multiple violations are treated as one.
كفارة (kaffārah): Penalty or expiation, specifically a dam (sacrifice of an animal) for ihram violations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a muhrim wears stitched clothes due to an enemy threat, and the enemy leaves, then another enemy arrives, how many dams are required?
A) One dam, because the need is continuous.
B) Two dams, because the first cause ended and a new cause began.
C) One dam, because the wearing is for the same purpose.
D) No dam, because it is a necessity.

Key Takeaway
The number of expiations for ihram violations is determined by the unity or multiplicity of the cause, not the frequency of the act.

Covering the Head in Ihram: Quarter vs Majority
Intra-Hanafi dispute on head covering threshold

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the minimum head covering that necessitates a dam and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Quarter suffices for dam in riwayah; majority is stronger in dirayah. Both are considered.

In the Hanafi school, the act of covering the head during ihram triggers a compensatory dam (blood sacrifice) only if the covered area reaches a certain threshold. The classical texts reveal an internal dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that covering a quarter of the head suffices, while Muhammad and Abu Yusuf require the majority. This lesson examines the evidence and practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is covering the head during ihram. If done deliberately, a dam is wajib. But how much coverage triggers it?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa considers a quarter of the head as the threshold. This is the transmitted ruling in al-Hidayah and followed by many later authorities.
3
State Muhammad and Abu Yusuf's position
Muhammad and in one narration Abu Yusuf require the majority of the head. Their reasoning is that the violation (jinaayah) is not complete without covering most of the head.
4
Examine the tarjih
The quarter is preponderant in transmission (rajih riwayah), while the majority is preponderant in legal reasoning (rajih dirayah). The text does not give a final decision but presents both.
5
Apply to a practical scenario
If a muhrim bandages his head with a bandage covering less than a quarter, only sadaqah is due; if it covers a quarter or more, a dam is due according to Abu Hanifa.
The scenario in al-Mabsut and al-Zahiriyyah is interpreted accordingly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في التغطية فانصرفت إلى الكامل وهو ما يغطى به عادة كالقلنسوة والعمامة فخرج ما لا يغطى به عادة كالطست والإجانة والعدل فلا شيء عليه وعلى هذا يفرع ما في الظهيرية : ما لو دخل المحرم تحت ستر الكعبة وإن كان يصيب وجهه ورأسه فهو مكروه لا شيء عليه وإلا فلا بأس به، وظاهر ما في المتون يقتضي أنه لا بد من تغطية جميع المأس في لزوم الدم، وما رأيته رواية ولهذا لم يصرحوا بحكم ما دونها وإنما المنقول عن الأصل اعتبار الربع ومشى عليه كثير واختاره في الظهيرية مقتصراً عليه وعزاه في الهداية إلى أنه عن أبي حنيفة، وعن محمد اعتبار الأكثر وهو مروي عن أبي يوسف أيضاً كما اعتبر أكثر اليوم في لزوم الدم، واختاره في فتح القدير من جهة الدراية. فالحاصل أن الربع راجح رواية والأكثر راجح دراية باعتبار أن تكامل الجناية لا يحصل بما دون الأكثر بخلاف حلق ربع الرأس فإنه معتاد. ويتفرع على هذا ما لو عصب رأسه بعصابة فعلى اعتبار الربع إن أخذت قدره من الرأس لزمه ،دم، وإن كان أقل فصدقة فما في المبسوط والظهيرية من أنه لو عصب يوماً فعليه صدقة محمول على ما إذا لم تأخذ قدر الربع أو مفرع على اعتبار الأكثر
And he spoke generally regarding covering, so it applies to complete covering, which is what one covers with customarily, like the cap and turban. Thus excluded is what one does not cover with customarily, like the basin, the tray, and the bundle, so nothing is due. Based on this, what is in al-Zahiriyyah is derived: if a muhrim enters under the covering of the Ka'bah, and if it touches his face and head, it is makruh and nothing is due; otherwise there is no harm. The apparent meaning of the texts requires that covering all the head is necessary for the obligation of blood. I have not seen this as a narration; therefore they did not state the ruling for less than that. But the transmitted from the original is consideration of a quarter, and many followed it, and al-Zahiriyyah chose it, limiting to it, and it is attributed in al-Hidayah to Abu Hanifah. From Muhammad is consideration of the majority, and it is also narrated from Abu Yusuf, as he considered the majority of the day in the obligation of blood. And he chose it in Fath al-Qadir from the perspective of insight. So the conclusion is that the quarter is preponderant in narration, and the majority is preponderant in insight, considering that the perfection of the violation is not achieved with less than the majority, contrary to shaving a quarter of the head, for it is customary. And based on this, if he bandages his head with a bandage: according to the consideration of the quarter, if it takes a quarter of the head, a dam is due; if less, then sadaqah. What is in al-Mabsut and al-Zahiriyyah that if he bandages for a day, sadaqah is due, is interpreted as when it does not take the quarter, or it is derived from the consideration of the majority.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the measure of head covering (taghtiyah) that necessitates a dam. The author first distinguishes between what is considered covering (like a cap or turban) and what is not (like a basin or tray). When a muhrim covers his head, the amount covered matters. The transmitted ruling from Abu Hanifa is that covering a quarter of the head triggers a dam. Muhammad and Abu Yusuf consider the majority of the head as the threshold. The author notes that the quarter is stronger in transmission (riwayah), while the majority is stronger in legal reasoning (dirayah) because the completion of the violation (takaamul al-jinaayah) does not occur with less than the majority. However, shaving a quarter of the head is different—it is customary and triggers a dam. An application: if one bandages the head with a bandage that covers less than a quarter, only sadaqah is due. The mufta bihi position leans towards the quarter in riwayah, but the majority is also considered in practice.

Key Terms
الربع (al-rubu'): Quarter of the head; the minimum area that, when covered, obligates a dam according to Abu Hanifa.
الأكثر (al-akthar): Majority of the head; the threshold according to Muhammad and Abu Yusuf for obligating a dam.

Key Terms from Source Passage
